<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41597"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A draft genome for Spatholobus suberectus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-04"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Spatholobus suberectus Dunn (S. suberectus), which belongs to the Leguminosae, is an important medicinal plant in China. Owing to its long growth cycle and increased use in human medicine, wild resources of S. suberectus have decreased rapidly and may be on the verge of extinction. De novo assembly of the whole S. suberectus genome provides us a critical potential resource towards biosynthesis of the main bioactive components and seed development regulation mechanism of this plant. Utilizing several sequencing technologies such as Illumina HiSeq X Ten, single-molecule real-time sequencing, 10x Genomics, as well as new assembly techniques such as FALCON and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C), we assembled a chromosome-scale genome about 798&#8201;Mb in size. In total, 748&#8201;Mb (93.73%) of the contig sequences were anchored onto nine chromosomes with the longest scaffold being 103.57&#8201;Mb. Further annotation analyses predicted 31,634 protein-coding genes, of which 93.9% have been functionally annotated. All data generated in this study is available in public databases.

Owing to its long growth cycle and increased use in human medicine, wild resources of S. suberectus have decreased rapidly and may be on the verge of extinction.                         </ul>
                    </div>
                </div>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Spatholobus suberectus Dunn</i> (<i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>), which belongs to the Leguminosae, is an important medicinal plant in China. Owing to its long growth cycle and increased use in human medicine, wild resources of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> have decreased rapidly and may be on the verge of extinction. <i>De novo</i> assembly of the whole <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome provides us a critical potential resource towards biosynthesis of the main bioactive components and seed development regulation mechanism of this plant. Utilizing several sequencing technologies such as Illumina HiSeq X Ten, single-molecule real-time sequencing, 10x Genomics, as well as new assembly techniques such as FALCON and chromatin interaction mapping (Hi-C), we assembled a chromosome-scale genome about 798âMb in size. In total, 748âMb (93.73%) of the contig sequences were anchored onto nine chromosomes with the longest scaffold being 103.57âMb. Further annotation analyses predicted 31,634 protein-coding genes, of which 93.9% have been functionally annotated. All data generated in this study is available in public databases.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Spatholobus suberectus</i> Dunn is widely used as a food supplement in tea, wine, and soup as well as being one of the most important Chinese medicinal plants (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig1">1a</a>) for treatment of various diseases such as blood stasis syndrome, abnormal menstruation, and rheumatism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Lee, M. H., Lin, Y. P., Hsu, F. L., Zhan, G. R. &amp; Yen, K. Y. Bioactive constituents of Spatholobus suberectus in regulating tyrosinase-related proteins and mRNA in HEMn cells. Phytochemistry 67, 1262â1270 (2006)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d31580e661">1</a></sup>. It is mainly distributed in Fujian, Guangdong, Yunnan Province, and the Guangxi Zhuang Autonomous Region of China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wu, Z. Y., Raven, P. H. &amp; Hong, D. Y. Flora of China. (Beijing: Science Press &amp; St. Louis: Missouri Botanical Garden Press, 2010)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d31580e665">2</a></sup>. The vine stem of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>, called âchicken blood vinesâ in China due to an outflow of red juice outflow when the vine stem is injured (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig1">1b</a>), is the critical medicinal component<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cui, Y. J., Liu, P. &amp; Chen, R. Y. Studies on the active constituents in vine stem of Spatholobus suberectus. Chin. J. Chin. Mater. Med 30, 121â123 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d31580e679">3</a></sup>. Pharmacological and clinical studies have demonstrated that <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> exhibits various functions against oxidation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Fu, Y. F. et al. Immunomodulatory and antioxidant effects of total flavonoids of Spatholobus suberectus Dunn on PCV2 infected mice. Sci. Rep 7, 8676 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d31580e689">4</a></sup>, viruses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chen, S. R. et al. In Vitro Study on Anti-Hepatitis C Virus Activity of Spatholobus suberectus Dunn. Molecules 21, 1367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d31580e693">5</a></sup>, bacteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Cho, H. et al. Spatholobus suberectus Dunn. constituents inhibit sortase A and Staphylococcus aureus cell clumping to fibrinogen. Arch. Pharm. Res. 40, 518â523 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d31580e698">6</a></sup>, cancer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Peng, F., Meng, C., Zhou, Q., Chen, J. &amp; Xiong, L. Cytotoxic Evaluation against Breast Cancer Cells of Isoliquiritigenin Analogues from Spatholobus suberectus and Their Synthetic Derivatives. J. Nat. Prod. 79, 248â251 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d31580e702">7</a></sup>, and platelets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Lee, B. J. et al. Antiplatelet effects of Spatholobus suberectus via inhibition of the glycoprotein IIb/IIIa receptor. J. Ethnopharmacol. 134, 460â467 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31580e706">8</a></sup>. The crud drug of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> is therefore used in many patented Chinese medicines, and the market demand for the wild resource is increasing rapidly. But unlike other Leguminosae plants, the seed setting rate of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> is low (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig1">1c</a>), and most of the fruit falls off before seed maturation, which results in a low natural reproductive capacity. The growth cycle of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> is very long and the crud drug must grow for more than seven years before it can be used as medicine. These factors have combined to decrease the wild resources of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> in China to the verge of extinction.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="467" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Morphological character of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>. (<b>a</b>) A picture of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> plant. (<b>b</b>) The vine stem of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> is called âchicken blood vinesâ. (<b>c</b>) The pod of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> has only one seed.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To investigate biosynthesis of the main bioactive components and seed development mechanism needed for future <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> production we generated a high-quality draft version of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome. Whole-genome sequencing of several species in Leguminosae plants have been performed, for instance, <i>Lotus japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome Structure of the Legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d31580e808">9</a></sup>, <i>Glycine max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31580e815">10</a></sup>, <i>Medicago truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520â524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31580e821">11</a></sup>, <i>Glycyrrhiza uralensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mochida, K. et al. Draft genome assembly and annotation of Glycyrrhiza uralensis, a medicinal legume. Plant J. 89, 181â194 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d31580e827">12</a></sup>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Gupta, S. et al. Draft genome sequence of Cicer reticulatum L., the wild progenitor of chickpea provides a resource for agronomic trait improvement. DNA Res. 24, 10 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31580e833">13</a></sup>, and <i>Cajanus cajan</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 83â89 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d31580e840">14</a></sup>, however, there are few reports of Subtribe Erythrininae Benth, containing nine genera of Leguminosae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wu, Z. Y., Raven, P. H. &amp; Hong, D. Y. Flora of China. (Beijing: Science Press &amp; St. Louis: Missouri Botanical Garden Press, 2010)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d31580e844">2</a></sup>. As one of the members of this subtribe, genomic information of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> can fill this gap.</p><p>The genome size of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>, a diploid (2nâ=â18) species, was estimated to be 793âMb using 17-mer frequency distribution analysis with SOAPdenovo. In this study, we combined sequences generated on the Illumina, PacBio, and 10X Genomics GemCode platform as well as the new assembly technique FALCON to generate the first draft genome assembly of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>. The assembled genome is about 798âMb with scaffold and contig N50 sizes of 6.9âMb and 2.1âMb, respectively. The <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> assembly was further refined using 233.19âGb Hi-C data: 748âMb (93.73%) of the contig sequences were anchored onto nine chromosomes, the scaffold N50 was improved to be 86.99âMb, and the longest scaffold was 103.57âMb.</p><p>Almost half of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome (47.82%) was occupied by repetitive elements, the largest amount of which was long terminal repeat retrotransposons (17.32%). Combined with homology-based predictions, <i>de novo</i> predictions and transcriptome-based predictions, 31,634 protein-coding genes with an average transcript size of 1,097.55âbp were predicted in the genome. In total, 93.9% (29,688) of protein-coding genes were successfully functionally annotated.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant materials and DNA extraction</h3><p><i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> samples from Nanning, Guangxi Zhuang Autonomous Region, China (22Â°51â²28â³N, 108Â°22â²2â³E) were selected for genome sequencing. The samples were kept at the Guangxi Botanical Garden of Medicinal Plants for breeding and research purposes. Total genomic DNA was isolated from fresh young leaves of 8-year-old <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> using the Plant DNA Kit (TIANGEN) according to the manufacturerâs instructions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>The DNA was sheared by a CovarisÂ® M220 focused-ultrasonicator<sup>TM</sup> (Covaris, Woburn, Massachusetts, USA). The sheared DNA, with fragment sizes of 250âbp and 450âbp, was processed using the TrueSeq DNA PCR-Free LT Library Kit protocol. PCR products were purified (AMPure XP system) and library quality was assessed on an Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100 system. These PCR-Free libraries were sequenced with a HiSeq X Ten instrument as 150âbp paired-end reads. In total, 77.73âGb of raw sequence data were generated (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 The sizes of sequencing data using various sequencing platforms.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Sheared DNA (40âÎ¼g) was purified and concentrated with AMPure PB beads (PacBio) and further used for SMRTbell preparation according to the manufacturerâs protocol (Pacific Biosciences; 20-kb template preparation using BluePippin (Sage Science) size selection system with a 15-kb cut-off). The libraries were then sequenced with a PacBio sequel instrument (Pacific Biosciences, Menlo 31 Park, CA, USA). A total of 11 SMRT Cells were used to yield 79.79-fold genome coverage of sequence data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab1">1</a>), consisting of 63.27âGb sequence data with an N50 read length of 14,288âbp (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of characteristics of Pacbio long-read.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The linked read sequencing libraries were constructed on a 10X Genomics GemCode platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Weisenfeld, N. I., Kumar, V., Shah, P., Church, D. M. &amp; Jaffe, D. B. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 27, 757â767 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1265">15</a></sup>. Sample indexing and partition barcoded libraries were prepared using the Chromium Genome Reagent Kit (10x Genomics) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. The barcode sequencing library was first quantified by Qubit2.0, insert size was checked using an Agilent2100, and finally quantified by qPCR. The 123.09âGb library was sequenced with 150âbp paired-end reads on an Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>For the Hi-C library, chromatin was fixed in place with formaldehyde in the nucleus. Fixed chromatin was digested with DpnII restriction endonuclease, 5â² overhangs were filled in with biotinylated nucleotides, and free blunt ends were ligated. After ligation, cross-links were reversed, and the DNA was purified from protein. Purified DNA was treated to remove biotin that was not internal to the ligated fragments. The DNA was then sheared to a mean fragment size of 350âbp, and sequencing libraries were generated using NEBNext Ultra enzymes and Illumina-compatible adaptors. Biotin-containing fragments were isolated using streptavidin beads before PCR enrichment of each library. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq platform to produce 233.19âGb Hi-C sequence data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab1">1</a>). The quality of Hi-C sequencing was evaluated using HiCUP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wingett, S. et al. HiCUP: pipeline for mapping and processing Hi-C. data. F1000Res. 4, 1310 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1278">16</a></sup>. The effect rate (%)â=âUnique di-tigs/Total Reads Processedâ=â4,356,614/10,000,000â=â43.57% (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab3">3</a>). Typically, 35.91% of the alignable read pairs represent interchromosomal interactions. Eleven percent represents intrachromosomal interactions between fragments less than 10âkb apart and 53.09% are intrachromosomal read pairs that are more than 10âkb apart (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Statistics of Hi-C sequencing and mapping.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Estimation of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome size</h3><p>Quality-filtered reads from the Illumina platform were subjected to 17-mer frequency distribution analysis with SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18â18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1557">17</a></sup>. K-mer 17 was selected to estimate the genome size and heterozygosity of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig2">2</a>). We plotted the distribution of k-mer depth against frequency with a main peak occurring at the depth of 40 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig2">2</a>). Based on the total number of k-mers (32,476,446,092), the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome size was calculated to be approximately 793.39âMb, using the following formula: genome sizeâ=âk-mer_Number/Peak_Depth and Revised Gsizeâ=âGenome sizeâÃâ(1-Error Rate). The heterozygosity of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome is 0.74%.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="625" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Estimation of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome size by K-mer analysis.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome assembly</h3><p><i>De novo</i> assembly of the 63.27âGb PacBio single-molecule long reads from SMRT Sequencing was performed using FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Chin, C. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1627">18</a></sup>. In order to get enough corrected reads, the longest 60 subreads were first selected as seed reads to do error correction. Then error-corrected reads were aligned to each other and assembled into genomic contigs using FALCON with parameters length_cutoff_prâ=â5000, max_diffâ=â120, max_covâ=â130. The draft assembly was polished using the quiver algorithm. Pilon was used to perform error correction of p-contigs with 98.02X coverage of short paired-end reads generated from Illumina HiSeq Platforms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0112963&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1631">19</a></sup>. The assembly consisted of 1,954 contigs, with a contig N50 length of 2.06âMb (total lengthâ=â794âMb) (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-5"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Summary of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome assembly using PacBio long reads.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We used BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at, &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d31580e1833">20</a></sup> to align the 10X Genomics data to the assembly using default settings. Scaffolding was performed by fragScaff (<i>in vitro</i>, long-range sequence information for <i>de novo</i> genome assembly via transposase contiguity) with the barcoded sequencing reads.</p><p>The assembly consisted of 1,146 scaffolds, with the scaffold N50 length improving to 6.9âMb (total lengthâ=â798âMb) and contig N50 of 2.1âMb (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab5">5</a>). The genome assembly size is similar to the estimated genome size by k-mer analysis.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-6"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Summary of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome assembly using PacBio long reads and 10X genomics data.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/tables/6"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The input <i>de novo</i> assembly, shotgun reads, and Dovetail Hi-C library reads were used as input data for HiRise, a software pipeline designed specifically for using proximity ligation data to scaffold genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Putnam, N. H. et al. Chromosome-scale shotgun assembly using an in vitro method for long-range linkage. Genome Res. 26, 342â350 (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2132">21</a></sup>. Shotgun and Dovetail Hi-C library sequences were aligned to the draft input assembly using a modified SNAP read mapper (<a href="http://snap.cs.berkeley.edu">http://snap.cs.berkeley.edu</a>). The separations of Dovetail Hi-C read pairs mapped within draft scaffolds were analyzed by HiRise to produce a likelihood model for genomic distance between read pairs, and the model was used to identify and break putative misjoins, score prospective joins, and make joins above a threshold. After scaffolding, shotgun sequences were used to close gaps between contigs.</p><p>The <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> assembly was further refined using 233.19âGb Hi-C data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Tab1">1</a>): 748âMb (93.73%) of the contig sequences were anchored onto nine chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig3">3</a>). The scaffold N50 was finally improved to be 86.99âMb and the longest scaffold was 103.57âMb.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="375" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Diagrammatic sketch of the annotation pipeline.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Indentification of repetitive elements in <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>
                        </h3><p>Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2192">22</a></sup> was employed to identify tandem repeats in the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome. RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) and RepeatProteinMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinf. 4, Unit 4.10 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2209">23</a></sup> were used against Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2214">24</a></sup> to identify known transposable element repeats. In addition, RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>), RepeatScout (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl 1), i351â358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2232">25</a></sup>, PILER (<a href="http://www.drive5.com/piler/">http://www.drive5.com/piler/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl 1), i152â158 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2243">26</a></sup>, and LTR_Finder (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder">http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/ltr_finder</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res 35, W265â268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2255">27</a></sup> were utilized to identify <i>de novo</i> evolved repeats (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig3">3</a>).</p><p>The combined results show that almost half of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome (47.82%) was occupied by repetitive elements (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig4">4bâe</a>). Among these, long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransponsons represent the largest amount of repetitive elements, reaching 17.32% of the genome, fewer than soybean (42%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2277">10</a></sup> and chickpea (46%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Varshney, R. K. et al. Draft genome sequence of chickpea (Cicer arietinum) provides a resource for trait improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 240â246 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2281">28</a></sup>, but are similar to <i>Lotus japonicus</i> (18%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome Structure of the Legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2289">9</a></sup>. LTR/Copia repeats were the most abundant, making up 10.06% of the genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig4">4d</a>), followed by LTR/Gypsy elements (6.61%; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig4">4e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0110-x/MediaObjects/41597_2019_110_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Circos Plot Showing the Genomic Features of <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i>. Concentric circles, from outermost to innermost, show (<b>a</b>) gene density (blue), (<b>b</b>) tandem repeats density (green), (<b>c</b>) transposon element density (purple), (<b>d</b>) LTR-Copia density (yellow), (<b>e</b>) LTR-Gypsy density (red) and intra-genome collinear blocks connected by curved lines. All distributions are drawn in a window size of 300âkb, chromosomes_ scaleâ=â5,000,000âbp.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene annotation</h3><p>Genes in the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome were annotated using multiple methods, including homology-based predictions, <i>de novo</i> predictions and transcriptome-based predictions (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig3">3</a>). For <i>de novo</i> predictions, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res 32, W309â312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2363">29</a></sup>, GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78â94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2367">30</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2371">31</a></sup>, geneid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; GuigÃ³, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinf. 18, Unit 4.3 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2375">32</a></sup> and SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2379">33</a></sup> analysis were performed on the repeat-masked genome, with parameters trained from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Predicted protein sequences from <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i> (ftp://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/refseq/plant/Nelumbo_nucifera/latest_assembly_versions/GCF_000365185.1_Chinese_Lotus_1.1 version 1.1), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-32/gff3/arabidopsis_thaliana/, version 10.32), <i>Glycine max</i> (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-32/fasta/glycine_max/dna/, version 1.0), <i>Petunia axillaris</i> (ftp://ftp.solgenomics.net/genomes/Petunia_axillaris/, version 1.6.2), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-32/fasta/solanum_lycopersicum/, release-32), and <i>Oryza sativa</i> (ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-32/fasta/oryza_sativa/, version 1.0) were used for homology-based predictions. First, query sequences were subjected to tblastn analysis with an Expect (E)-value cutoff of 1e-5. BLAST hits corresponding to reference proteins were concatenated by Solar software, and low-quality records were removed. The genomic sequence of each reference protein was extended upstream and downstream by 2,000âbp to represent a protein-coding region. Gene structures contained in each protein region were predicted using GeneWise software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Birney, E. &amp; Durbin, R. Using GeneWise in the Drosophila annotation experiment. Genome Res 10, 547â548 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2406">34</a></sup>. For transcriptome-based predictions, RNA from five organs (root, petiole, leaves, flowers, and stems) was isolated and RNA-seq data were used for gene annotation, processed by TopHat and Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2410">35</a></sup>. RNA-seq data were also assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494â1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2414">36</a></sup>. PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2418">37</a></sup> software (<a href="http://pasapipeline.github.io/">http://pasapipeline.github.io/</a>) was then used to generate a full transcriptome-based genome annotation. The homology, <i>de novo</i>, and transcriptomic gene sets were merged to form a comprehensive and non-redundant reference gene set using EVidenceModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2433">38</a></sup> software. Next, PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654â5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2437">37</a></sup> was used to generate UTRs as suggested by the RNA-seq data.</p><p>Our analysis indicates that 31,634 protein-coding genes with an average transcript size of 1,097.55âbp were predicted in the genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#Fig4">4a</a>).</p><p>Functional annotation of the protein-coding genes was carried out using blastp (E-value cut-off 1e<b>-</b>05) against SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bairoch, A. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2453">39</a></sup> and NR databases. Protein domains were annotated by searching against InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Mulder, N. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterPro and InterProScan: tools for protein sequence classification and comparison. Methods Mol. Biol 396, 59â70 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2457">40</a></sup> and Pfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Punta, M. et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 263â266 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2461">41</a></sup>, using InterProScan and HMMER (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org">http://hmmer.janelia.org</a>), respectively. The GO terms for genes were obtained from the corresponding InterPro or Pfam entry. The pathways in which the genes might be involved were assigned by BLAST against the KEGG database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2473">42</a></sup> with the E-value cut-off of 1e-05.</p><p>Overall, 79% (24,976), 70.8% (22,394), and 82.5% (26,082) of genes showed enrichment in InterPro, KEGG, and GO respectively. In total, 93.9% (29,688) of protein-coding genes were successfully annotated for conserved functional motifs or functional terms.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Non-coding RNA annotation</h3><p>Annotation of tRNA was performed using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2488">43</a></sup> software with default parameters. rRNA annotation was based on homology with rRNAs from several diverse higher plant species (not shown), using blastn with âE-valueâ=â1e-5â. miRNA and snRNA genes were predicted by INFERNAL software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335â1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2492">44</a></sup> using the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2496">45</a></sup>.</p><p>The final results included 820 miRNA, 672 tRNA, 261 rRNA, and 550 snRNA with average lengths of 117.33, 75.32, 305.41 and 115.50âbp respectively.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>This Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession QUWT00000000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Qin, S. S. et al. Draft genome of Spatholobus suberectus. GenBank, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:QUWT00000000&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2512">46</a></sup>. The version described in this paper is version QUWT01000000. Raw read files are available at NCBI Sequence Read Archive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    https://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP157950&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2516">47</a></sup>. All the annotation tables containing results of an analysis of the draft genome are available at figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Qin, S. S. et al. Draft genome of Spatholobus suberectus. figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.c.4414709.v1&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2520">48</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Evaluation of the completeness of the <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome assembly</h3><p>To estimate the quality of genome assembly, short reads were mapped back to the consensus genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. (Oxford University Press, 2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2543">49</a></sup> and an overall 97.29% mapping rate was found, suggesting that our assembly results contained comprehensive genomic information. Gene region completeness was evaluated by RNA-Seq data (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#MOESM2">S1</a>): of the 53,538 transcripts assembled by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494â1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2550">36</a></sup>, 99.62% could be mapped to our genome assembly, and 95.94% were considered as complete (more than 90% of the transcript could be aligned to one continuous scaffold).</p><p>The completeness of gene regions was further assessed using CEGMA (conserved core eukaryotic gene mapping approach)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2557">50</a></sup>: 240 of 248 (96.77%) conserved core eukaryotic genes from CEGMA were captured in our assembly, and 206 (83.06%) of these were complete (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#MOESM2">S2</a>). Furthermore, we performed BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d31580e2564">51</a></sup> analysis based on a benchmark of 956 conserved plant genes, of which 96% had complete gene coverage (including 18% duplicated ones), 1% were fragmented and only 2.6% were missing (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0110-x#MOESM2">S3</a>). These data largely support a high quality <i>S</i>. <i>suberectus</i> genome assembly, which can be used for further investigation.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code Availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code Availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The execution of this work involved many software tools, whose versions, settings and parameters are described below.</p>
              <p><b>(1) SOAPdenovo:</b> version 3.0, default parameters; <b>(2) FALCON:</b> version 3.1, length_cutoff_prâ=â5000, max_diffâ=â120, max_covâ=â130; <b>(3) HiCUP:</b> version 0.5.10, <b>(4) HiRise:</b> Dovetail Genomics LLC, Santa Cruz, CA, USA; <b>(5) BWA:</b> version 0.7.8, default parameters; <b>(6) Tandem Repeat Finder:</b> version 409, default parameters; <b>(7) RepeatMasker:</b> version 4.0.5, default parameters; <b>(8) Repbase:</b> version 15.02; <b>(9) RepeatModeler:</b> version 1.0.11, default parameters; <b>(10) RepeatScout:</b> version 1.0.5, default parameters; <b>(11) PILER:</b> version 1.06, default parameters; <b>(12) LTR_FINDER:</b> version 1.0.7, default parameters; <b>(13) Augustus:</b> version 3.0.2, default parameters; <b>(14) GENSCAN:</b> version 1.0, default parameters; <b>(15) geneid:</b> version 1.4, default parameters; <b>(16) GlimmerHMM:</b> version 3.0.2, default parameters; <b>(17) SNAP:</b> version 11-29-2013; <b>(18) BLAST:</b> version 2.2.26, default parameters; <b>(19) GeneWise:</b> version 2.2.0, default parameters; <b>(20) TopHat:</b> version 2.0.8, default parameters; <b>(21) Cufflinks:</b> version 2.1.1, default parameters; <b>(22) Trinity:</b> version 2.4.0, default parameters; <b>(23) PASA:</b> version 2.3.3, default parameters; <b>(24) EVidenceModeler:</b> version 1.1.1, default parameters; <b>(25) InterPro:</b> version 5.16, default parameters; <b>(26) Pfam database:</b> version 03-30-2016; <b>(27) InterProScan:</b> version 4.8, default parameters; <b>(28) NR database:</b> version 08-10-2015; <b>(29) KEGG database:</b> version 08-31-2015; <b>(30) SwissProt database:</b> version 05-24-2016; <b>(31) HMMER:</b> version 3.1b1, default parameters; <b>(32) tRNAscan-SE:</b> version 1.3.1, default parameters; <b>(33) BUSCO</b>: version 3.0.2, Embryophyta Version odb9; <b>(34) CEGMA:</b> version 2.5.</p>
